<div id="review_244164183" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/244164183" />
    <a title="Kate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2130215-kate"><img alt="Kate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1390849801p2/2130215.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/244164183?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate" href="/user/show/2130215-kate">Kate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780060168704" href="/book/show/158742.The_Living">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2130215-kate?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer244164183" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14664562375788971032">"<i>The Living</i> is a vivid, populous, old-fashioned novel about the Pacific Northwest frontier.<br/><br/>"Bellingham Bay lies ninety miles north of Seattle, on the northwest coast of Washington State. A rough settlement founded in the 1850s became the town of Whatcom. <i>The Living</i> tells the rich and serious story of nineteenth century Whatcom.<br/><br/>"Here is the intimate, murderous tale of three men. Clare Fishburn believes that greatness lies in store for him. John Ireland Sharp, an educated orphan, abandons hope whe</span>
  <span id="freeText14664562375788971032" style="display:none">"<i>The Living</i> is a vivid, populous, old-fashioned novel about the Pacific Northwest frontier.<br /><br />"Bellingham Bay lies ninety miles north of Seattle, on the northwest coast of Washington State. A rough settlement founded in the 1850s became the town of Whatcom. <i>The Living</i> tells the rich and serious story of nineteenth century Whatcom.<br /><br />"Here is the intimate, murderous tale of three men. Clare Fishburn believes that greatness lies in store for him. John Ireland Sharp, an educated orphan, abandons hope when he sees socialists expel the Chinese workers from the region. Beal Obenchain, who lives in a cedar stump, threatens Clare Fishburn's life.<br /><br />"Durable, open-hearted women live on these shores, among them Minta Honer, of Baltimore, who raises hops and endures every misfortune, and Ada Tawes, who sees the region's hopes rise and fall. Lummi and Nooksack Indian people fish and farm; explorers climb into the Cascade Mountains to survey. Men struggle to clear the forests; hermits pay their debts in sockeye salmon; miners track gold-bearing streams. A killer lashes a Chinese worker to a wharf piling at low tide. Settlers launch a boat and sing by a beach fire. People give birth, drown, burn, inherit rich legacies, and commit expensive larcenies. On Madrone Island the beauty of sky and sea gives the illusion of peace. In the upriver settlement of Goshen, the dark trees come down and children grow up on farms spread under the light. Settlers pour into Whatcom, the harbor town, to catch the boom the railroads bring. All this takes place a hundred years ago, when these vital, ruddy men and women were 'the living.' "<br />~~front &amp; back flaps<br /><br />Epic! How come they haven't made a movie out of this? Or a tv series? It has all the ingredients: gorgeous scenery, wonderful characters, subplot upon subplot. It could go on forever!! I'm glad they haven't -- this is a wonderful book, and it would be terribly altered and diminished if Hollywood got their hands on it.<br /><br />What mesmerized me was the glimpse in everyday life in those times. We forget how fragile life was -- how easily &amp; quickly lost. We forget that feeding a family was a daily struggle to wrest food from the land, and forget that the least variation in weather or circumstances meant there was nothing to eat. I was fascinated to see how differently people needed to think about their lives in order to survive -- what different strengths they needed to master than we do today.<br /><br />And as soon as enough progress had been made that there were towns, and a budding political system -- the same graft and greed and venality as is writ so large in our political systems today. Contrasted by good men, honest men -- most of whom are not strong enough to make much of a difference outside their own small little sphere.<br /><br />I enjoyed this book far more than I thought I would. I like the author's work, but wasn't prepared to find this one a page turner. And it is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14664562375788971032" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1242080295" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1242080295" />
    <a title="Arun Croll" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8690410-arun-croll"><img alt="Arun Croll" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567654911p2/8690410.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1242080295?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arun Croll" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arun Croll" href="/user/show/8690410-arun-croll">Arun Croll</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1242080295" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13077941968233652418">I've read all of Annie Dillard's books of prose and "The Living" is my least favorite by far.<br/><br/>I slogged through this novel because I'm a huge fan of Dillard's writing style. Fortunately, she doesn't disappoint on that level. The gorgeous imagery is dispensed by means of carefully constructed sentences that are full of rich language and clever juxtapositions. I highlighted her well-phrased insights and laughed out loud multiple times, but not often enough considering the novel's size and pace—both</span>
  <span id="freeText13077941968233652418" style="display:none">I've read all of Annie Dillard's books of prose and "The Living" is my least favorite by far.<br /><br />I slogged through this novel because I'm a huge fan of Dillard's writing style. Fortunately, she doesn't disappoint on that level. The gorgeous imagery is dispensed by means of carefully constructed sentences that are full of rich language and clever juxtapositions. I highlighted her well-phrased insights and laughed out loud multiple times, but not often enough considering the novel's size and pace—both of which are comparable to the gigantic, old growth trees that the book painstakingly describes.<br /><br />Alas, "The Living" is underwhelming as a story—even by the standards of literary fiction. The summary on the book jacket describes all the action you can expect to see. The story might appeal to those who enjoy sprawling epics—emphasis on "sprawl" rather than "epic." In my opinion, there are far too many characters, few of whom are proactive enough to truly come alive or meet a surprising end. If you were to ask me "What's it about?" the best answer I could give would be, "It's about some settlers living in Washington during the 19th century."<br /><br />After finishing this book, I'm left with some knowledge about the time period, an appreciation for the value of cookie-cutter Hollywood story structure, and a feeling of relief. I can only recommended "The Living" to readers who are fans of Dillard or atmospheric historical fiction.<br /><br />If you've never read Annie Dillard before, I recommend starting with "Teaching a Stone to Talk," whose concise essays will quickly give you a taste of what she does best.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13077941968233652418" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_272833002" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/272833002" />
    <a title="Jessika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1166992-jessika"><img alt="Jessika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547989302p2/1166992.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/272833002?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessika" href="/user/show/1166992-jessika">Jessika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780060168704" href="/book/show/158742.The_Living">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jessika?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jessika?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1166992-jessika?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer272833002" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15980753373702493539">As a fan of Annie Dillard, I enjoyed reading this one. Anyone expecting a pageturner might as well turn the other way, but I don't necessarily mean that to be a bad thing. This is a slow, languorous read; this is one you will want to sink into for a while and soak up. If you've read anything by Annie Dillard before, you sort of know what to expect here. Her writing is beautiful and it's worth it to take note of it. At times, her metaphors can be rather thick and heavy, but she forces you to pay </span>
  <span id="freeText15980753373702493539" style="display:none">As a fan of Annie Dillard, I enjoyed reading this one. Anyone expecting a pageturner might as well turn the other way, but I don't necessarily mean that to be a bad thing. This is a slow, languorous read; this is one you will want to sink into for a while and soak up. If you've read anything by Annie Dillard before, you sort of know what to expect here. Her writing is beautiful and it's worth it to take note of it. At times, her metaphors can be rather thick and heavy, but she forces you to pay attention as you're reading. You have to slow down to really enjoy this one. This is a story that follows multiple generations of the early pioneers of the West Coast. It is literally a story about living--about the lives of her characters, nothing more, nothing less. There is no overarching plot, and that was actually alright with me. Being that this book is about 400 pages long (with small font and margins), by the end of it, I will admit, I was ready for a change of pace, but that does not mean that I didn't enjoy this. I continue to be a fan of Annie Dillard, and if you're looking for a story with an interesting (and endearing) cast of characters where you can really sink into the writing, I'd recommend this one. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15980753373702493539" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_197912432" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/197912432" />
    <a title="Jenny Grundberg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/131529-jenny-grundberg"><img alt="Jenny Grundberg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1183698184p2/131529.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/197912432?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny Grundberg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny Grundberg" href="/user/show/131529-jenny-grundberg">Jenny Grundberg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer197912432" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4237086618558451498">What I loved:<br/><br/>1) Again, I'm a sucker for a way-out-west pioneer story. Just love 'em. And the more settlement details the better.<br/><br/>2) Chocked full of historical tidbits of the Pacific Northwest. Made me want to move there right now (so what if I'm a few centuries late...)<br/><br/>3) The stark unromanticism of Dillard's story-telling. It made everything feel very authentically harsh and unforgiving---pretty apropo for the setting.<br/><br/>What I hated:<br/><br/>1) Like another reviewer, I wasn't all that interested in ANY of t</span>
  <span id="freeText4237086618558451498" style="display:none">What I loved:<br /><br />1) Again, I'm a sucker for a way-out-west pioneer story. Just love 'em. And the more settlement details the better.<br /><br />2) Chocked full of historical tidbits of the Pacific Northwest. Made me want to move there right now (so what if I'm a few centuries late...)<br /><br />3) The stark unromanticism of Dillard's story-telling. It made everything feel very authentically harsh and unforgiving---pretty apropo for the setting.<br /><br />What I hated:<br /><br />1) Like another reviewer, I wasn't all that interested in ANY of the characters, and I felt like Dillard wasn't terribly passionate about any of them either.<br /><br />2) Not enough dialouge for my taste. I need a little human interaction to understand the characters' motives.<br /><br />3) Beal Oberchain. I thought he was going to be a real, honest to goodness trouble-making nutter. He turned out to just be a somewhat irritating nutter. I never fully understood Clare's reaction to Beal's threat. I felt like it was a bit contrived and not terribly genuine. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4237086618558451498" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_33269801" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1331243-christine"><img alt="Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1218076059p2/1331243.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33269801?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christine" href="/user/show/1331243-christine">Christine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33269801" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15489309873561743144">This piece of historical fiction really took perseverence! It's a lengthy story set in the mid- to late- 1800's, about settlers to the Puget Sound area of Washington. I learned a lot about that place and time--relations between white settlers and native americans and then later, Chinese immigrants, the density and enormity of the timber and the difficulties that posed, the impact of the economic crashes of that period, and just how precarious life was in that wilderness... but Dillard doesn't cr</span>
  <span id="freeText15489309873561743144" style="display:none">This piece of historical fiction really took perseverence! It's a lengthy story set in the mid- to late- 1800's, about settlers to the Puget Sound area of Washington. I learned a lot about that place and time--relations between white settlers and native americans and then later, Chinese immigrants, the density and enormity of the timber and the difficulties that posed, the impact of the economic crashes of that period, and just how precarious life was in that wilderness... but Dillard doesn't create characters that you really grow to care about. Interesting enough to keep me going, but I can't say this book really added anything to my life! Maybe I found it all a little too grim. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15489309873561743144" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer64465915" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18367490492470442886">This book offered a non-sentimental perspective of the settlement of the West by Europeans. Behind the complex plot full of many intertwining stories, there was a philosophical commentary on how life is defined by death. I read this book because my library branch didn't have anything by Willa Cather, and I was in the mood for a book about this time period. I read "An American Childhood" years ago, and I am very glad I had this book in hand. <br/><br/>It did take awhile to become engrossed in the plot. Wit</span>
  <span id="freeText18367490492470442886" style="display:none">This book offered a non-sentimental perspective of the settlement of the West by Europeans. Behind the complex plot full of many intertwining stories, there was a philosophical commentary on how life is defined by death. I read this book because my library branch didn't have anything by Willa Cather, and I was in the mood for a book about this time period. I read "An American Childhood" years ago, and I am very glad I had this book in hand. <br /><br />It did take awhile to become engrossed in the plot. With stories about multiple families across a couple of generations, it took some time for the story to knit itself together.</span>

<div id="review_2485824580" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2485824580" />
    <a title="Jay Reimer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14612640-jay-reimer"><img alt="Jay Reimer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484590375p2/14612640.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2485824580?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jay Reimer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jay Reimer" href="/user/show/14612640-jay-reimer">Jay Reimer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2485824580" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12403910350546896057">I enjoy Annie Dillard's eclectic writings (like For the Time Being) but this was my first novel. True to her style the content and narrative are unexpected. I lived the setting (I live near Bellingham, Washington now) and the story includes unexpected characters and events. I did not research it's plausibility, but it sure reads as "authentic!" This novel kept me a fan: now I love Dillard in 2 modes: non-fiction-reflective and fictional novel.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8xTVO56eYhBx9WmKBakxlCzEragMvY4eUVMr9BeG+d9g+kQYaYnZAtKSDlcvJYUMWBQVMdBd/sXNUKBPQZW/RA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lfOUw9SNVGDhze6aHT8SlfjS6l43/refo+5CER0Ex3sGHQXgI5rvckKqiUc3s6YNjAJSx+sex0Q/7cmqSxeB4A==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Crazy Horse, perhaps the <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13715555752733916456\" style=\"display:none\">On the Rez is a sharp, unflinching account of the modern-day American Indian experience, especially that of the Oglala Sioux, who now live on the Pine Ridge Indian Reservation in the plains and badlands of the American West. Crazy Horse, perhaps the greatest Indian war leader of the 1800s, and Black Elk, the holy man whose teachings achieved worldwide renown, were Oglala; in these typically perceptive pages, Frazier seeks out their descendants on Pine Ridge--a/k/a the rez--which is one of the poorest places in America today.\n\n\n\nAlong with his longtime friend Le War Lance (whom he first wrote about in his 1989 bestseller, Great Plains) and other Oglala companions, Frazier fully explores the rez as they visit friends and relatives, go to pow-wows and rodeos and package stores, and tinker with a variety of falling-apart cars. He takes us inside the world of the Sioux as few writers ever have, writing with much wit, compassion, and imagination. In the career of SuAnne Big Crow, for example, the most admired Oglala basketball player of all time, who died in a car accident in 1992, Frazier finds a contemporary reemergence of the death-defying, public-spirited Sioux hero who fights with grace and glory to save her followers.\n\nOn the Rez vividly portrays the survival, through toughness and humor, of a great people whose culture has helped Lark and Termite is set during the 1950s in West Virginia and Korea. It is a story of the power of loss and love, the echoing ramifications of war, family secrets, dreams and ghosts, and the unseen, almost magical bonds that unite and sustain us.\n\nAt its center, two children: Lark, on the verge of adulthood, and her brother, Termite, a child unable to walk and talk but filled with radiance. Around them, their mother, Lola, a haunting but absent presence; their aunt Nonie, a matronly, vibrant woman in her fifties, who raises them; and Termite\'s father, Corporal Robert Leavitt, who finds himself caught up in the chaotic early months of the Korean War.\n\nTold with deep feeling, the novel invites us to enter into the hearts and thoughts of the leading characters, even into Termite\'s intricate, shuttered consciousness. We are with Leavitt, trapped by friendly fire alongside the Korean children he tries to rescue. We see Lark\'s dreams for Termite and her own future, and how, with the aid of a childhood love and a spectral social worker, she makes them happen. We learn of Lola\'s love for her soldier husband and her children and unravel the mystery of her relationship with Nonie. We discover the lasting connections between past and future on the night the town experiences an overwhelming flood, and we follow Lark and Termite as their lives are changed forever.<\/span>\n  <a The protagonist of Apex Hides the Hurt is a nomenclature consultant. If you want just the right name for your new product, whether it be automobile or antidepressant, sneaker or spoon, he’s the man to get the job done. Wardrobe lack pizzazz? Come to the Outfit Outlet. Always the wallflower at social gatherings? Try Loquacia. And of course, whenever you take a fall, reach for Apex, because Apex Hides the Hurt. Apex is his crowning achievement, the multicultural bandage that has revolutionized the adhesive bandage industry. “Flesh-colored” be damned—no matter what your skin tone is—Apex will match it, or your money back.After leaving his job (following a mysterious misfortune), his expertise is called upon by the town of Winthrop. Once there, he meets the town council, who will try to sway his opinion over the coming days. Lucky Aberdeen, the millionaire software pioneer and hometown-boy-made-good, wants the name changed to something that will reflect the town’s capitalist aspirations, attracting new businesses and revitalizing the community. Who could argue with that? Albie Winthrop, beloved son of the town’s aristocracy, thinks Winthrop is a perfectly good name, and can’t imagine what the fuss is about. Regina Goode, the mayor, is a descendent of the black settlers who founded the town, and has her own secret agenda for what the name should be. Our expert must decide the outcome, with all its implications for the town’s future. Which name will he choose? Or perhaps he will devise his own? And what’s with his limp, anyway?Apex Hides the Hurt brilliantly and wryly satirizes our contemporary culture, where memory and history are subsumed by the tides of marketing.<\/span>\n  <a First published in Norway as two books in 1924 and 1925, the author collaborated with Minnesotan Lincoln Colcord on the English translation.\n\nT<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12270244608760554473\" style=\"display:none\">Giants in the Earth (Norwegian: Verdens Grøde) is a novel by Norwegian-American author Ole Edvart Rølvaag. First published in Norway as two books in 1924 and 1925, the author collaborated with Minnesotan Lincoln Colcord on the English translation.\n\nThe novel follows a Norwegian family\'s struggles as they try to make a new life as pioneers in the Dakota territory. Rølvaag is interested in psychology and the human cost of empire building, at a time when other writers focused on the glamor and romance of the West. The book reflects his personal experiences as a settler as well as the immigrant homesteader experience of his wife’s family. Both the grim realities of pioneering and the gloomy fatalism of the Norse mind are captured in depictions of snow storms, locusts, poverty, hunger, loneliness, homesickness, the difficulty of fitting into a new culture, and the estrangement of immigrant children who grow up in a new land. It is a novel at once palpably European and distinctly American.\n\nGiants in the Earth was turned into an opera by Douglas Moore and Arnold Sundgaard; it won the Pulitzer Prize for Music in 1951.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"12270244608760554473\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer Few people are equipped to recognize what is happening because the culture of the Western world has been deformed by a widespread undermining of moral absolutes.Against this background, the Delaney family of Swiftcreek, British Columbia, is struck a severe blow when the father of the family, the editor of a small newspaper which dares to speak the truth, is arrested by the dreaded Office of Internal Security. The reader will take away from this disturbing book a number of urgent questions: Are we living in the decisive moment of history? How dire is our situation? Do we live in pessimistic dread, or a Christian realism founded on hope? The Wolverine is the key to opening up Alaska and its huge reserves of gold to the outside world, but previous attempts have ended in tragedy.\n\nFor Forrester, the decision to accept this mission is even more difficult, as he is only recently married to Sophie, the wife he had perhaps never expected to find. Sophie is pregnant with their first child, and does not relish the prospect of a year in a military barracks while her husband embarks upon the journey of a lifetime. She has genuine cause to worry about her pregnancy, and it is with deep uncertainty about what their future holds that she and her husband part.\n\nA story shot through with a darker but potent strand of the magic that illuminated The Snow Child, and with the sweep and insight that characterizes Rose Tremain\'s The Colour, this novel from Pulitzer Prize finalist Eowyn Ivey singles her out as a He became a respected research biochemist with an anti-theistic worldview to bolster his work--and he had no intention of seeking a God he didn\'t believe in. That is, until the very science he loved led him to question the validity of an atheistic worldview. His journey to answer the questions that confronted him drew him into becoming a fully committed Christian, determined to show others the truth: modern science doesn\'t contradict God at all but instead supports Christianity.\n\nIn the first half of the book, Sy begins with how his experiences and quest for knowledge as a student and early in his career brought him to question his materialist assumptions. He goes on to reveal how lessons from physics, biology, and human nature--all presented for lay readers to easily understand--actually argue for belief in God. In the second half of the book, Sy looks at the arguments often presented against God in academic and scientific settings and explains the false foundations on which they rest.\n\nFor those who have been told that the realities of science call for a rejection of God--but can\'t quite get rid of the feeling that this shouldn\'t be true--The Works of His Hands is an ideal reminder that the two don\'t have to be bitter enemies.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5209.Annie_Dillard">About Annie Dillard</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6867043988512085464">Annie Dillard (born April 30, 1945) is an American author, best known for her narrative prose in both fiction and non-fiction. She has published works of poetry, essays, prose, and literary criticism, as well as two novels and one memoir. Her 1974 work Pilgrim at Tinker Creek won the 1975 Pulitzer Prize for General Nonfiction. Dillard taught for 21 years in the English department of Wesleyan Unive</span>
  <span id="freeText6867043988512085464" style="display:none">Annie Dillard (born April 30, 1945) is an American author, best known for her narrative prose in both fiction and non-fiction. She has published works of poetry, essays, prose, and literary criticism, as well as two novels and one memoir. Her 1974 work Pilgrim at Tinker Creek won the 1975 Pulitzer Prize for General Nonfiction. Dillard taught for 21 years in the English department of Wesleyan University, in Middletown, Connecticut.<br /><br />(from Wikipedia)</span>
